This contrasts against the modern understanding of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_ecology" title="Theoretical ecology">ecological theory</a> where varieties are viewed as the real phenomena of interest and having a role in the origins of adaptations by means of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Odum05_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Benson00_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benson00-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sober80_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sober80-223">[223]</a></sup> Early conceptions of ecology, such as a balance and regulation in nature can be traced to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (died <i>c</i>. 425 BC), who described one of the earliest accounts of <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a> in his observation of "natural dentistry". Basking <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a>, he noted, would open their mouths to give <a href="/wiki/Sandpiper" title="Sandpiper">sandpipers</a> safe access to pluck <a href="/wiki/Leech" title="Leech">leeches</a> out, giving nutrition to the sandpiper and oral hygiene for the crocodile.<sup id="cite_ref-Egerton01_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egerton01-221">[221]</a></sup> Aristotle was an early influence on the philosophical development of ecology. He and his student <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> made extensive observations on plant and animal migrations, biogeography, physiology, and on their behaviour, giving an early analogue to the modern concept of an ecological niche.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes85_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes85-224">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hughes75_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes75-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ecological concepts such as food chains, population regulation, and productivity were first developed in the 1700s, through the published works of microscopist <a href="/wiki/Antoni_van_Leeuwenhoek" class="mw-redirect" title="Antoni van Leeuwenhoek">Antoni van Leeuwenhoek</a> (1632–1723) and botanist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bradley_(botanist)" title="Richard Bradley (botanist)">Richard Bradley</a> (1688?–1732).<sup id="cite_ref-Odum05_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-5">[5]</a></sup> Biogeographer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> (1769–1859) was an early pioneer in ecological thinking and was among the first to recognize ecological gradients, where species are replaced or altered in form along <a href="/wiki/Environmental_gradient" title="Environmental gradient">environmental gradients</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">cline</a> forming along a rise in elevation. Humboldt drew inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> as he developed a form of "terrestrial physics". In Newtonian fashion, he brought a scientific exactitude for measurement into natural history and even alluded to concepts that are the foundation of a modern ecological law on species-to-area relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-Kingsland04_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingsland04-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosenzweig03_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenzweig03-227">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hawkins01_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawkins01-228">[228]</a></sup> Natural historians, such as Humboldt, <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> (among others) laid the foundations of the modern ecological sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup> The term "ecology" (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Oekologie, Ökologie</i></span>) is of a more recent origin and was first coined by the German biologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> in his book <i>Generelle Morphologie der Organismen</i> (1866). Haeckel was a zoologist, artist, writer, and later in life a professor of comparative anatomy.<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer57_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer57-230">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Friederichs58_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friederichs58-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>By ecology, we mean the whole science of the relations of the organism to the environment including, in the broad sense, all the "conditions of existence. "Thus the theory of evolution explains the housekeeping relations of organisms mechanistically as the necessary consequences of effectual causes and so forms the <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> groundwork of ecology.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Ernst Haeckel (1866)<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer57_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer57-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:140</sup> <sup id="ref_B_1" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_B">[B]</a></sup></cite></div>
<div class="thumb tleft" style="background:#f9f9f9; padding:5px; border:1px solid gray; margin:0.5em; font-size:11px;">
<p><a href="/wiki/File:Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Nicola Perscheid - Ernst Haeckel.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg/90px-Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg" width="90" height="122" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg/135px-Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg/180px-Nicola_Perscheid_-_Ernst_Haeckel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="966" data-file-height="1308" /></a> <a href="/wiki/File:Warming,Eugen-c1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Warming,Eugen-c1900.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg/90px-Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg" width="90" height="124" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg/135px-Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg/180px-Warming%2CEugen-c1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="209" data-file-height="287" /></a></p>
<div style="border: none; width:180px;">
<div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Eugenius_Warming" title="Eugenius Warming">Eugenius Warming</a> (right), two founders of ecology</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Opinions differ on who was the founder of modern ecological theory. Some mark Haeckel's definition as the beginning;<sup id="cite_ref-Hinchman07_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hinchman07-232">[232]</a></sup> others say it was <a href="/wiki/Eugenius_Warming" title="Eugenius Warming">Eugenius Warming</a> with the writing of <a href="/wiki/Plantesamfund" title="Plantesamfund">Oecology of Plants: An Introduction to the Study of Plant Communities</a> (1895),<sup id="cite_ref-Goodland75_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodland75-233">[233]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a>' principles on the economy of nature that matured in the early 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Egerton07_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egerton07-234">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kormandy78_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kormandy78-235">[235]</a></sup> Linnaeus founded an early branch of ecology that he called the economy of nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Egerton07_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egerton07-234">[234]</a></sup> His works influenced Charles Darwin, who adopted Linnaeus' phrase on the <i>economy or polity of nature</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_Species" class="mw-redirect" title="The Origin of Species">The Origin of Species</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer57_230-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer57-230">[230]</a></sup> Linnaeus was the first to frame the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_nature" title="Balance of nature">balance of nature</a> as a testable hypothesis. Haeckel, who admired Darwin's work, defined ecology in reference to the economy of nature, which has led some to question whether ecology and the economy of nature are synonymous.<sup id="cite_ref-Kormandy78_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kormandy78-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG/220px-Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG" width="220" height="101" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG/330px-Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG/440px-Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1034" data-file-height="477" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Darwin_EcoExperiment.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The layout of the first ecological experiment, carried out in a grass garden at <a href="/wiki/Woburn_Abbey" title="Woburn Abbey">Woburn Abbey</a> in 1816, was noted by Charles Darwin in <i>The Origin of Species</i>. The experiment studied the performance of different mixtures of species planted in different kinds of soils.<sup id="cite_ref-Hector02_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hector02-236">[236]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair26_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair26-237">[237]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From Aristotle until Darwin, the natural world was predominantly considered static and unchanging. Prior to <i>The Origin of Species</i>, there was little appreciation or understanding of the dynamic and reciprocal relations between organisms, their adaptations, and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Benson00_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benson00-222">[222]</a></sup> An exception is the 1789 publication <i>Natural History of Selborne</i> by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a> (1720–1793), considered by some to be one of the earliest texts on ecology.<sup id="cite_ref-May99_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-May99-238">[238]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> is mainly noted for his treatise on evolution,<sup id="cite_ref-Darwin_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darwin-239">[239]</a></sup> he was one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Soil_ecology" title="Soil ecology">soil ecology</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Meysman06_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meysman06-240">[240]</a></sup> and he made note of the first ecological experiment in <i>The Origin of Species</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hector02_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hector02-236">[236]</a></sup> Evolutionary theory changed the way that researchers approached the ecological sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-Acot97_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acot97-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Nowhere can one see more clearly illustrated what may be called the sensibility of such an organic complex,--expressed by the fact that whatever affects any species belonging to it, must speedily have its influence of some sort upon the whole assemblage. He will thus be made to see the impossibility of studying any form completely, out of relation to the other forms,--the necessity for taking a comprehensive survey of the whole as a condition to a satisfactory understanding of any part.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Stephen Forbes (1887)<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes1887_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1887-242">[242]</a></sup></cite></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1900">Since 1900</span></h3>
<p>Modern ecology is a young science that first attracted substantial scientific attention toward the end of the 19th century (around the same time that evolutionary studies were gaining scientific interest). Notable scientist <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Swallow_Richards" title="Ellen Swallow Richards">Ellen Swallow Richards</a> may have first introduced the term "<a href="/wiki/Oekology" class="mw-redirect" title="Oekology">oekology</a>" (which eventually morphed into <a href="/wiki/Home_economics" title="Home economics">home economics</a>) in the U.S. as early 1892.<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 20th century, ecology transitioned from a more <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">descriptive form</a> of <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a> to a more <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">analytical form</a> of <i>scientific natural history</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kingsland04_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingsland04-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Clements" title="Frederic Clements">Frederic Clements</a> published the first American ecology book in 1905,<sup id="cite_ref-Clements05_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clements05-244">[244]</a></sup> presenting the idea of plant communities as a <a href="/wiki/Superorganism" title="Superorganism">superorganism</a>. This publication launched a debate between ecological holism and individualism that lasted until the 1970s. Clements' superorganism concept proposed that ecosystems progress through regular and determined stages of <a href="/wiki/Seral_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Seral development">seral development</a> that are analogous to the developmental stages of an organism. The Clementsian paradigm was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Gleason" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Gleason">Henry Gleason</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Simberloff80_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simberloff80-245">[245]</a></sup> who stated that ecological communities develop from the unique and coincidental association of individual organisms. This perceptual shift placed the focus back onto the life histories of individual organisms and how this relates to the development of community associations.<sup id="cite_ref-Gleason26_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gleason26-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Clementsian superorganism theory was an overextended application of an <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealistic form</a> of holism.<sup id="cite_ref-Levins80_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levins80-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wilson88_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson88-105">[105]</a></sup> The term "holism" was coined in 1926 by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Christiaan_Smuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan Christiaan Smuts">Jan Christiaan Smuts</a>, a South African general and polarizing historical figure who was inspired by Clements' superorganism concept.<sup id="cite_ref-Foster08_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster08-247">[247]</a></sup><sup id="ref_C_1" class="reference"><a href="#cnote_C">[C]</a></sup> Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sutherland_Elton" title="Charles Sutherland Elton">Charles Elton</a> pioneered the concept of food chains in his classical book <i>Animal Ecology</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Elton27_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton27-81">[81]</a></sup> Elton<sup id="cite_ref-Elton27_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elton27-81">[81]</a></sup> defined ecological relations using concepts of food chains, food cycles, and food size, and described numerical relations among different functional groups and their relative abundance. Elton's 'food cycle' was replaced by 'food web' in a subsequent ecological text.<sup id="cite_ref-Allee32_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allee32-248">[248]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_J._Lotka" title="Alfred J. Lotka">Alfred J. Lotka</a> brought in many theoretical concepts applying thermodynamic principles to ecology.</p>
<p>In 1942, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Lindeman" title="Raymond Lindeman">Raymond Lindeman</a> wrote a landmark paper on the <a href="/wiki/Trophic_dynamics#Trophic_dynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Trophic dynamics">trophic dynamics</a> of ecology, which was published posthumously after initially being rejected for its theoretical emphasis. Trophic dynamics became the foundation for much of the work to follow on energy and material flow through ecosystems. <a href="/wiki/Robert_MacArthur" title="Robert MacArthur">Robert MacArthur</a> advanced mathematical theory, predictions, and tests in ecology in the 1950s, which inspired a resurgent school of theoretical mathematical ecologists.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cook77_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook77-249">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Odum68_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odum68-250">[250]</a></sup> Ecology also has developed through contributions from other nations, including Russia's <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Vernadsky" title="Vladimir Vernadsky">Vladimir Vernadsky</a> and his founding of the biosphere concept in the 1920s<sup id="cite_ref-Ghilarov95_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ghilarov95-251">[251]</a></sup> and Japan's <a href="/wiki/Kinji_Imanishi" title="Kinji Imanishi">Kinji Imanishi</a> and his concepts of harmony in nature and habitat segregation in the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-It.C3.B491_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-It.C3.B491-252">[252]</a></sup> Scientific recognition of contributions to ecology from non-English-speaking cultures is hampered by language and translation barriers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ghilarov95_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ghilarov95-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>This whole chain of poisoning, then, seems to rest on a base of minute plants which must have been the original concentrators. But what of the opposite end of the food chain—the human being who, in probable ignorance of all this sequence of events, has rigged his fishing tackle, caught a string of fish from the waters of Clear Lake, and taken them home to fry for his supper?</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Rachel Carson (1962)<sup id="cite_ref-Carson62_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carson62-253">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:48</sup></cite></div>
<p>Ecology surged in popular and scientific interest during the 1960–1970s <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental movement</a>. There are strong historical and scientific ties between ecology, environmental management, and protection.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup> The historical emphasis and poetic naturalistic writings advocating the protection of wild places by notable ecologists in the history of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aldo_Leopold" title="Aldo Leopold">Aldo Leopold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Tansley" title="Arthur Tansley">Arthur Tansley</a>, have been seen as far removed from urban centres where, it is claimed, the concentration of pollution and environmental degradation is located.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palamar08_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palamar08-254">[254]</a></sup> Palamar (2008)<sup id="cite_ref-Palamar08_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palamar08-254">[254]</a></sup> notes an overshadowing by mainstream environmentalism of pioneering women in the early 1900s who fought for urban health ecology (then called <a href="/wiki/Euthenics" title="Euthenics">euthenics</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-243">[243]</a></sup> and brought about changes in environmental legislation. Women such as <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Swallow_Richards" title="Ellen Swallow Richards">Ellen Swallow Richards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julia_Lathrop" title="Julia Lathrop">Julia Lathrop</a>, among others, were precursors to the more popularized environmental movements after the 1950s.</p>
<p>In 1962, marine biologist and ecologist <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson" title="Rachel Carson">Rachel Carson</a>'s book <i><a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">Silent Spring</a></i> helped to mobilize the environmental movement by alerting the public to toxic <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/DDT" class="mw-redirect" title="DDT">DDT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulating</a> in the environment. Carson used ecological science to link the release of environmental toxins to human and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_health" title="Ecosystem health">ecosystem health</a>. Since then, ecologists have worked to bridge their understanding of the degradation of the planet's ecosystems with environmental politics, law, restoration, and natural resources management.<sup id="cite_ref-Hammond09_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond09-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh85_229-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh85-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palamar08_254-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palamar08-254">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krebs99_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs99-255">[255]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Laferri.C3.A8reStoett2003-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Laferri.C3.A8reStoett2003_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Eric Laferrière; Peter J. (1999). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hillnet.com/silent_spring.pdf">"The second Silent Spring"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. <i>Nature</i>. <b>400</b> (6745): 611–612. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1999Natur.400..611K">1999Natur.400..611K</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1038%2F23127">10.1038/23127</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEcology&amp;rft.atitle=The+second+Silent+Spring&amp;rft.au=Bradbury%2C+R.+B.&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+R.&amp;rft.aulast=Krebs&amp;rft.au=Siriwardena%2C+G.+M.&amp;rft.au=Wilson%2C+J.+D.&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.hillnet.com%2Fsilent_spring.pdf&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1999Natur.400..611K&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2F23127&amp;rft.issue=6745&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.pages=611-612&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=400" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
